        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1070275050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5396812605688177841">Horizon wasn't as frustrating a read as Passages, but I still didn't like it half as much as the first two books in the Sharing Knife series. Horizon feels repetitive. While Passages was a boat trip down the river, Horizon was a horse trip back up North. It was better than Passages (mostly because of the absence of Wit), but not terribly interesting, and some of the things I would have found most interesting (such as <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[how Dag teaches the half-Lakewalkers to use their ground, and ho</span></span>
  <span id="freeText5396812605688177841" style="display:none">Horizon wasn't as frustrating a read as Passages, but I still didn't like it half as much as the first two books in the Sharing Knife series. Horizon feels repetitive. While Passages was a boat trip down the river, Horizon was a horse trip back up North. It was better than Passages (mostly because of the absence of Wit), but not terribly interesting, and some of the things I would have found most interesting (such as <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[how Dag teaches the half-Lakewalkers to use their ground, and how that differs from a full Lakewalker <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) were simply skipped over.<br /><br />While I found Dag's experiments with ground interesting to a point, the Sharing Knife series never really explored much of what I found interesting about the idea of ground. The first two books seemed to hint that there was something important about how malices were created. I hoped that the second two would involve finding out how malices were created, or perhaps continue the escalation of the first two and rid the world of malices entirely. <br /><br />(As an aside, this books contains what is certainly the creepiest moment of the series, in which Fawn <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[is buried alive and nearly wakes up like that. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I found the end of Horizon unbelievable. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Bujold has just spent the majority of three books showing how stubborn the Lakewalkers are, and how they refuse to change in the face of common sense. But somehow, the existence of magic shield acorns changed the relationships between Lakewalkers and farmers nearly overnight. It didn't make sense to me. Four books have shown the antagonistic relationship between the two, and much has been made of how hard it is to change their minds, but suddenly Dag is able to bring everyone around. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Although I enjoyed the first two books in the series, I was quite disappointed by the last two. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_72720798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72720798" />
    <a title="Hester" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2658780-hester"><img alt="Hester" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72720798?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hester" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hester" href="/user/show/2658780-hester">Hester</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658780-hester?shelf=women-s-issues">women-s-issues</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658780-hester?shelf=american-writers">american-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658780-hester?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658780-hester?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658780-hester?shelf=minnesota-writers">minnesota-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72720798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18180037289713944320">I have mixed feelings about this series. I HATED the first book; it struck me as an annoying romance. I trust the author, though, so I read the rest of the series and was happily surprised to see how it dealt with so many different issues.<br/><br/>Like the Vorkosigan series, the Sharing Knife series contains elements of Midwestern history and culture in her new world. The series is about reconciling cultures and about how the domestic interacts with the extraordinary. It is about the dynamics of power; h</span>
  <span id="freeText18180037289713944320" style="display:none">I have mixed feelings about this series. I HATED the first book; it struck me as an annoying romance. I trust the author, though, so I read the rest of the series and was happily surprised to see how it dealt with so many different issues.<br /><br />Like the Vorkosigan series, the Sharing Knife series contains elements of Midwestern history and culture in her new world. The series is about reconciling cultures and about how the domestic interacts with the extraordinary. It is about the dynamics of power; how does a famous man keep the balance of power his housewife mate? Since I just read "The Meaning of Wife," this theme seemed to be hitting me over the head. It is about the importance of daily chores as well as of epic battles. Looking over the series as a whole, I find its scope ambitious. I am not sure I am one hundred percent happy with how these themes were tackled, but I admire McMaster Bujold for trying. These are themes people rarely take seriously, even though they dominate so many lives.<br /><br />My feelings about the ending reflect my feelings about the entire series. The protagonist couple, Fawn and Dag, have been trying to find a place for the two of them that fits within both their worlds, and failing miserably. I was surprised that McMaster believably found them one. And, as in the rest of the series, the solution depended on both Dag's status as possibly the most gifted man on the planet, and Fawn's status as helpful mate. They could not have done it without her, and it is not trite. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18180037289713944320" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/03g9TqnBPyrodPzzA1tV4c0FN0upiLFNpVqAge2nAACMNRywsth1AHWmqoNBnOafcim8lU8qLhtTs6mxnUQvA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HGLTKHOX4A/UjUCo6OUO46Rju1A+ueqPGmJS34/O077hH+evi/uFJ376CfEp7hAuXp8Jf0UjYPJBufZ7Tg1fAg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9151913242012821137">Lois McMaster Bujold was born in 1949, the daughter of an engineering professor at Ohio State University, from whom she picked up her early interest in science fiction. She now lives in Minneapolis, and has two grown children. <br/><br/>Her fantasy from HarperCollins includes the award-winning Chalion series and the Sharing Knife tetralogy; her science fiction from Baen Books features the perennially bestse</span>
  <span id="freeText9151913242012821137" style="display:none">Lois McMaster Bujold was born in 1949, the daughter of an engineering professor at Ohio State University, from whom she picked up her early interest in science fiction. She now lives in Minneapolis, and has two grown children. <br /><br />Her fantasy from HarperCollins includes the award-winning Chalion series and the Sharing Knife tetralogy; her science fiction from Baen Books features the perennially bestselling Vorkosigan Saga. Her work has been translated into over twenty languages.<br /><br />A listing of her awards and nominations may be seen here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sfadb.com/Lois_McMaster_Bujold">http://www.sfadb.com/Lois_McMaster_Bu...</a><br /> <br />A listing of her interviews is here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://vorkosigan.wikia.com/wiki/Author_Interviews">http://vorkosigan.wikia.com/wiki/Auth...</a><br /><br />An older fan-run site devoted to her work, The Bujold Nexus, is here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.dendarii.com/">http://www.dendarii.com/</a><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9151913242012821137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
